import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
import math


class MSARowSelfAttention(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, msa_dim, num_heads):
        super().__init__()
        self.num_heads = num_heads
        self.head_dim = msa_dim // num_heads
        self.norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim)
        self.q = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)
        self.k = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)
        self.v = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)
        self.out = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim)

    def forward(self, msa):
        B, S, N, D = msa.shape
        h, d = self.num_heads, self.head_dim
        x = self.norm(msa)
        q = self.q(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)
        k = self.k(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)
        v = self.v(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)
        attn = (q @ k.transpose(-1, -2)) / math.sqrt(d)
        attn = F.softmax(attn, dim=-1)
        out = (attn @ v).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).reshape(B, S, N, D)
        return self.out(out)


class OuterProductMeanSmall(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, msa_dim, pair_dim, hidden=32):
        super().__init__()
        self.norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim)
        self.a = nn.Linear(msa_dim, hidden)
        self.b = nn.Linear(msa_dim, hidden)
        self.out = nn.Linear(hidden * hidden, pair_dim)

    def forward(self, msa):
        x = self.norm(msa)
        a = self.a(x)
        b = self.b(x)
        outer = torch.einsum("bsih,bsjk->bijhk", a, b)
        S = msa.shape[1]
        outer = outer / max(S, 1)
        B, N1, N2, H1, H2 = outer.shape
        return self.out(outer.reshape(B, N1, N2, H1 * H2))


class TriMulOut(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, pair_dim, hidden):
        super().__init__()
        self.norm = nn.LayerNorm(pair_dim)
        self.pa = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden)
        self.pb = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden)
        self.ga = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden)
        self.gb = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden)
        self.norm_out = nn.LayerNorm(hidden)
        self.out = nn.Linear(hidden, pair_dim)
        self.gate_out = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim)

    def forward(self, pair):
        x = self.norm(pair)
        a = self.pa(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.ga(x))
        b = self.pb(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gb(x))
        o = torch.einsum("bikh,bjkh->bijh", a, b)
        o = self.out(self.norm_out(o))
        return torch.sigmoid(self.gate_out(pair)) * o


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Full Evoformer block from AlphaFold2 combining MSA row self-attention,
    outer-product mean update, and triangular multiplicative update of the
    pair representation.  A single block of the iterative refinement stack.
    """

    def __init__(self, msa_dim, pair_dim, num_heads, tri_hidden):
        super().__init__()
        self.msa_attn = MSARowSelfAttention(msa_dim, num_heads)
        self.opm = OuterProductMeanSmall(msa_dim, pair_dim)
        self.tri_mul = TriMulOut(pair_dim, tri_hidden)

    def forward(
        self, msa_repr: torch.Tensor, pair_repr: torch.Tensor
    ) -> tuple:
        """
        Args:
            msa_repr:  (B, S, N, msa_dim)
            pair_repr: (B, N, N, pair_dim)
        Returns:
            (updated_msa, updated_pair)
        """
        msa_repr = msa_repr + self.msa_attn(msa_repr)
        pair_repr = pair_repr + self.opm(msa_repr)
        pair_repr = pair_repr + self.tri_mul(pair_repr)
        return msa_repr, pair_repr


msa_dim = 64
pair_dim = 64
num_heads = 4
tri_hidden = 32
seq_len = 32
msa_depth = 8
batch_size = 2


def get_inputs():
    msa = torch.randn(batch_size, msa_depth, seq_len, msa_dim)
    pair = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, seq_len, pair_dim)
    return [msa, pair]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [msa_dim, pair_dim, num_heads, tri_hidden]

But coding all that in one kernel is quite involved. Let's break down each component. ### MSARowSelfAttention Input: msa (B, S, N, D) Operations: 1. LayerNorm on last dim. 2. Q = msa @ W_q^T (bias=False) 3. K = msa @ W_k^T 4. V = msa @ W_v^T 5. Reshape and permute: (B, S, N, H, d) -> (B, S, H, N, d) 6. QK^T: (B, S, H, N, d) @ (B, S, H, d, N) -> (B, S, H, N, N) 7. Scale by 1/sqrt(d) 8. Softmax over last dim (N) 9. Attn @ V: (B, S, H, N, N) @ (B, S, H, N, d) -> (B, S, H, N, d) 10. Permute back: (B, S, N, H, d) -> (B, S, N, D) 11. Out = out @ W_out^T + b_out All of this operates on MSA. ### OuterProductMeanSmall Input: msa (after msa_attn residual add) (B, S, N, D) Operations: 1. LayerNorm on last dim. 2. a = msa @ W_a^T + b_a 3. b = msa @ W_b^T + b_b 4. outer = einsum("bsih,bsjk->bijhk", a, b) This means for each batch b, for each i (N) and j (N), and for each h1 (H_opm) and h2 (H_opm): outer[b, i, j, h1, h2] = sum_{s=0..S-1} a[b, s, i, h1] * b[b, s, j, h2] 5. divide by max(S,1) 6. reshape to (B, N, N, H_opm*H_opm) 7. out = outer @ W_out^T + b_out ### TriMulOut Input: pair_repr (B, N, N, P) Operations: 1. LayerNorm on last dim. 2. pa = pair @ W_pa^T + b_pa 3. ga = pair @ W_ga^T + b_ga 4. pb = pair @ W_pb^T + b_pb 5. gb = pair @ W_gb^T + b_gb 6. a = pa * sigmoid(ga) 7. b = pb * sigmoid(gb) 8. o = einsum("bikh,bjkh->bijh", a, b) This means for each batch b, for each i (N), j (N), h (H_tri): o[b, i, j, h] = sum_{k=0..N-1} a[b, i, k, h] * b[b, j, k, h] Note: The original einsum is "bikh,bjkh->bijh". a is (B, N, N, H_tri), b is (B, N, N, H_tri). So sum over the second index of a and second index of b, which is k (the common dimension). Wait: in a: b i k h, b: b j k h -> output b i j h. So sum over k (sequence dimension). Yes. 9. norm_out(o) -> LayerNorm on last dim. 10. out_linear = o @ W_out^T + b_out 11. gate = sigmoid(pair @ W_gate^T + b_gate) 12. final = gate * out_linear Then residual connections: msa_repr += msa_attn_out pair_repr += opm_out pair_repr += tri_mul_out ### Goal We need to produce numerically equivalent outputs using custom CUDA kernel(s). We can compute per (b, s, n) thread: compute LN, then Q, K, V by dot product with weights. D=64, so each dot product is 64x64? No, Q = x @ W_q^T, where x is (D,) and W_q is (D, D). So for each position, we need to compute D outputs, each a dot product of length D. This is essentially a GEMV of size D x D. With D=64, we can have each thread compute one element of Q (i.e., one output channel). So 64 threads per position. Or we can have a warp per position, with each thread computing a chunk. But we have B*S*N = 2*8*32 = 512 positions. If we use 64 threads per position, total threads = 32768, which is enough to saturate GPU. Similarly for K and V. Then we need to compute QK^T for each (b, s, h). Q and K are reshaped to (h, d). d=16. QK^T size N x N = 32 x 32. For each (b, s, h), we have a 32x32 matrix multiplication of Q (N, d) and K^T (d, N). That's tiny. But we can compute it with one thread block per (b,s,h). Number of such blocks: B*S*H = 2*8*4 = 64. Each block computes a 32x32 matrix. We can use shared memory for K and Q tiles. Then softmax across rows of 32 elements. Each row can be computed by a warp or by the whole block. Then attn @ V: again 32x32 x 32x16 = 32x16. For each (b,s,h), compute output. Then output GEMM: reshape back to (B,S,N,D) and apply W_out. Again GEMV D x D per position. This is a lot but doable in a single kernel or split. Given complexity, maybe we can use a simpler approach: write a kernel that loops over the dimensions, using registers, and compute everything sequentially within a thread. Each block has H * N = 4*32 = 128 threads. Each thread handles one head and one query position. It loops over d_head=16 to compute Q, K, V? Wait Q, K, V are computed from x (D=64) and W (D,D). We can compute Q for a given head and position by reading W_q for that head's channels. Since each thread has a fixed (h, n), it can compute Q[b,s,n,h,:] by loading x[b,s,n,:] into registers/shared memory, then looping over d_head and D. That's 16*64=1024 multiplies per thread for Q. Similarly for K and V: 3*1024 = 3072 multiplies. With 128 threads per block, 16 blocks, total multiplies = 16*128*3072 = 6.3M, which matches FLOP. This is feasible. Then QK^T: each thread has Q (d_head=16) and needs to compute dot product with K for all N=32 keys. So for each key position k, it loops over d_head to compute dot(Q, K_k). That's 32*16 = 512 multiplies per thread. Then softmax: compute max and sum over 32 elements. Use warp shuffle for reduction. Then attn@V: each thread has attention weights (32) and needs to compute weighted sum of V_k for each d_head dimension. That's 32*16 = 512 multiplies. Then output linear: each thread now has output vector of size d_head=16. But the output linear is applied to concatenated heads: D=64. So we need to compute out = attn_out @ W_out^T + b_out. For a given position (b,s,n), the output is D=64. We can have each thread compute one output channel, so 64 threads per position. But currently we have 128 threads per (b,s). Maybe we can reorganize: use 64 threads per (b,s,n). Since N=32, that would be 2048 threads per (b,s). Maybe too many but okay. Or we can have each of the 128 threads compute a subset of the output channels. PyTorch will call cuBLAS for Q, K, V (batch matmul shape [B*S*N, D] x [D, D] -> [B*S*N, D]). Then reshape and bmm for attention: [B*S*H, N, d] x [B*S*H, d, N] -> [B*S*H, N, N]. Then softmax, then bmm with V. Then output linear. These cuBLAS calls are very fast. It might be hard to beat with custom kernels because cuBLAS is highly optimized even for small sizes, especially with TF32/FP16 etc. But our reference is FP32. However, we have to match FP32. In PyTorch, this einsum may be slow because it has to materialize a large intermediate or use a loop. Let's think: a is (B, S, N, H_opm), b is (B, S, N, H_opm). Output outer is (B, N, N, H_opm, H_opm). The contraction is over S. PyTorch's einsum engine will likely reshape to [B, S, N*H] and do something? Actually, the contraction over S with outer over the rest. The optimal path might be to do a tensordot over S: for each (i, h1) and (j, h2), sum_s a[b,s,i,h1]*b[b,s,j,h2]. This is essentially a batched matrix multiplication where batch dims are (B, i, h1, j, h2)? Not straightforward. PyTorch might implement it via a loop over S, multiplying and accumulating. But maybe it uses bmm after reshaping. Let's see: a can be viewed as [B*N*H, S], b as [B*N*H, S]? No, the indexing is different: for each b,i,j,h1,h2, sum over s of a[b,s,i,h1] * b[b,s,j,h2]. If we transpose a to [B, N, H, S] and b to [B, N, H, S], then for each b, we need outer product between a's (i,h1,:) and b's (j,h2,:). This is like a batched matrix multiplication of a reshaped as [B, N*H, S] and b reshaped as [B, S, N*H] -> [B, N*H, N*H]. Let's check: a_reshaped = a.permute(0,2,3,1).reshape(B, N*H, S). b_reshaped = b.permute(0,2,3,1).reshape(B, N*H, S). Then a_reshaped @ b_reshaped.transpose(-1,-2) gives [B, N*H, N*H]. Then reshape to [B, N, H, N, H] -> permute to [B, N, N, H, H]. Yes! So PyTorch einsum might detect this and use bmm. That would be very fast: [B, M, S] x [B, S, M] where M = N*H = 32*32 = 1024. So it's a bmm of size [2, 1024, 8] x [2, 8, 1024] -> [2, 1024, 1024]. This is FLOP = 2*1024*1024*8*2 = ~33M. Actually M=1024, S=8, B=2. So 2 * 1024 * 1024 * 8 = 16.7M multiplies, same as before. bmm of [2,1024,8] x [2,8,1024] is a GEMM of size (1024, 8) x (8, 1024). This is memory-bound because the inner dimension is small. So PyTorch einsum may call bmm but it's not super efficient. A custom kernel could do better by fusing with the previous linears and the division by S and the next linear. Similarly, TriMulOut einsum "bikh,bjkh->bijh": a is (B, N, N, H). We can think of it as for each b and h, compute A_{b,h} @ B_{b,h}^T where A_{b,h} is (N,N) and B_{b,h} is (N,N). Actually, a: [B, N, N, H], b: [B, N, N, H]. For fixed b and h, o_{ij} = sum_k a_{ik} b_{jk}. That's like A @ B^T where A is (N,N) and B is (N,N). So for each (b,h), it's a matmul of (N,N) x (N,N)^T? Wait: A[b,h] is (N,N) with rows i, cols k. B[b,h] is (N,N) with rows j, cols k. Then o[b,h] = A[b,h] @ B[b,h]^T. Output is (N,N). So overall it's a bmm: [B*H, N, N] x [B*H, N, N] -> [B*H, N, N] with second operand transposed. So bmm of size [64, 32, 32] x [64, 32, 32] -> [64, 32, 32] with transposed B. This is a batch of small matmuls. PyTorch may handle it via bmm, which is okay but not perfect. To get speedup, we should heavily fuse the OPM and TriMul operations, because those einsums with small inner dimensions might be memory-bound and we can avoid intermediate writes. The MSA attention part is already well-optimized in PyTorch (cuBLAS + softmax). It might be hard to beat significantly, but we can fuse the residual add and maybe the output. However, the biggest opportunity is fusing the entire OuterProductMeanSmall and TriMulOut sequences into single kernels. For OPM, we can do: - Input msa (B,S,N,D) - For each (b, i, j), compute output directly. Because the operation is: LN -> a = linear_a(msa), b = linear_b(msa) -> outer over S -> /S -> reshape -> out_linear. We can fuse the linear_a and linear_b into the loop. PyTorch reference operations (approx kernel count): 1. MSA attn: - LN: 1 kernel - 3 Linear: 3 kernels (GEMM) - view/permute: no kernel - QK bmm: 1 kernel - scale: maybe fused with QK - softmax: 1 kernel - attn@V bmm: 1 kernel - permute/reshape: no kernel - out Linear: 1 kernel - residual add: 1 kernel Total ~10 kernels. 2. OPM: - LN: 1 - 2 Linear: 2 - einsum: likely multiple kernels (reshape + bmm) - div: 1 - reshape: 0 - out Linear: 1 - residual add: 1 Total ~7 kernels. 3. TriMul: - LN: 1 - 4 Linear: 4 - 2 sigmoid: 2 (or fused with linear? PyTorch doesn't fuse automatically) - 2 elementwise muls: 2 - einsum: ~1-3 kernels - LN: 1 - Linear: 1 - gate Linear + sigmoid + mul with pair: maybe 3 kernels - residual add: 1 Total ~15+ kernels. So reference forward has ~30+ kernel launches. If we write a kernel for OPM+TriMul fused, that's 1 kernel launch for pair update. And keep MSA attn as PyTorch (10 kernel launches). Total ~11 launches. That's a big reduction. And if our custom pair kernel is efficient, we could get a good speedup. But we can do even better: fuse MSA attn into a custom kernel too, bringing total to 2 kernel launches. Given the time, maybe the sweet spot is: - Kernel 1: `msa_attn` (custom but simple) - Kernel 2: `pair_fused` (custom and parallel) Let's design Kernel 2 first, since it's likely the bottleneck and easier to parallelize. ### Kernel 2: Fused Pair Update (OPM + TriMulOut) Input: - msa: (B, S, N, D) - pair: (B, N, N, P) - weights for OPM: W_a (D, H_opm), b_a (H_opm), W_b (D, H_opm), b_b (H_opm), W_opm_out (H_opm*H_opm, P), b_opm_out (P). Also gamma_opm (D), beta_opm (D) for LN. - weights for TriMul: gamma_pair (P), beta_pair (P) for first LN, W_pa (P, H_tri), b_pa, W_ga, b_ga, W_pb, b_pb, W_gb, b_gb. Then norm_out gamma (H_tri), beta (H_tri), W_out_tri (H_tri, P), b_out_tri. W_gate (P, P), b_gate. - Output: updated pair (B, N, N, P) We want to compute for each (b, i, j) the updated pair. Because OPM and TriMul are sequential (TriMul reads pair after OPM residual), we can compute OPM contribution first, add to pair, then compute TriMul contribution, add to pair. But we can also compute TriMul using the original pair? Wait, residual is pair = pair + opm_out, then pair = pair + tri_mul_out. TriMulOut takes the updated pair as input (pair after OPM add). So we need pair + opm_out before computing tri_mul. If we compute per (b,i,j) block: 1. Load pair_orig[b,i,j,:] into registers. 2. Compute opm_out[b,i,j,:] using msa. 3. pair_tmp = pair_orig + opm_out. 4. Compute tri_mul_out[b,i,j,:] using pair_tmp. 5. Write pair_final = pair_tmp + tri_mul_out. But tri_mul_out depends on the entire i-row and j-row of pair_tmp (for the einsum). That means we need pair_tmp for all k in dimension N. If each block only computes one (i,j), it needs to load pair_tmp[b, i, k, :] for all k, and pair_tmp[b, j, k, :] for all k. But pair_tmp is only computed locally for (i,j). To get pair_tmp for other k, we'd need either: - Compute opm_out for all k first (global), then compute tri_mul. - Or compute opm_out for the i-row and j-row on the fly inside the block. But opm_out for (i,k) and (j,k) requires reading msa. Since msa is global and read-only after MSA attention, we can compute a_{b,s,i,:} and a_{b,s,j,:} etc. on the fly without needing to store OPM output globally. Wait, opm_out for (i,j) depends on a_{b,s,i,:} and b_{b,s,j,:}. For TriMul, we need pair_lin for (i,k) and (j,k). pair_lin is pair + opm_out. opm_out for (i,k) depends on a_{b,s,i,:} and b_{b,s,k,:}. So if we want to compute TriMul for (i,j) on the fly, we need to compute opm_out for all (i,k) and (j,k) inside the block. That is possible but costly: for each k (32), compute opm_out for (i,k), which is a linear(outer_product_mean(a_i, b_k)). The outer product mean is size H_opm*H_opm = 1024. So computing opm_out per k is expensive (1024 FLOP for outer + 1024*64 FLOP for linear). Doing that for all k (32) inside each block would be 32 * (1024 + 65536) = 2.1M FLOP per block. Times 2048 blocks = 4.3B FLOP, which is way more than needed because opm_out is computed once per (i,j), not per each block for TriMul. This is wasteful. So we must compute OPM globally first, store it, then compute TriMul. So we need at least two kernels for pair: one for OPM, one for TriMul. Unless we compute OPM for all (i,j) in one kernel, then in the same kernel compute TriMul after a grid synchronization. But CUDA doesn't support global sync within a kernel except by splitting into multiple kernels (or using cooperative groups, which is tricky). So we'll use two kernels. Kernel 2a: OPM Kernel 2b: TriMul Alternatively, we can compute OPM and add residual in kernel 2a. Then kernel 2b reads updated pair, computes tri_mul_out, adds residual. Total kernels: 3. Let's design them. #### OPM Kernel For each (b, i, j), we need to compute: x_msa = LayerNorm(msa[b, :, i, :])? Wait, OPM norm is applied on the last dimension (msa_dim) across all positions? Let's check OuterProductMeanSmall code: ```python def forward(self, msa): x = self.norm(msa) a = self.a(x) b = self.b(x) outer = torch.einsum("bsih,bsjk->bijhk", a, b) S = msa.shape[1] outer = outer / max(S, 1) B, N1, N2, H1, H2 = outer.shape return self.out(outer.reshape(B, N1, N2, H1 * H2)) ``` `self.norm` is `nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim)`. It normalizes over the last dimension (msa_dim). But `msa` has shape (B,S,N,D). So `self.norm(msa)` normalizes over D for every (b,s,n). So each position is normalized independently. Then a = self.a(x) where x is (B,S,N,D). a is (B,S,N,H_opm). Similarly b. Then einsum "bsih,bsjk->bijhk". For fixed (b,i,j), it sums over s: a[b,:,i,:] * b[b,:,j,:] -> outer shape (H_opm, H_opm). Then divide by S. Then reshape to (B,N,N,H_opm*H_opm) and apply self.out (Linear from H_opm*H_opm to pair_dim). The linear is per position (i,j). So for OPM, we can parallelize over (b,i,j). Total 2048 positions. Each position requires: - For each s (0..7), compute a_s = W_a @ x_{b,s,i} + b_a and b_s = W_b @ x_{b,s,j} + b_a. Wait, x_{b,s,i} is D=64. a_s is H=32. So each linear is 64*32 = 2048 FLOP. 2 linears per s = 4096 FLOP. For S=8, that's 32k FLOP. - Then compute outer sum over s: for each h1, h2, sum_s a_s[h1] * b_s[h2]. That's H*H*S = 32*32*8 = 8192 multiplies. - Then divide by S. - Then linear out: H*H -> P. That's 1024*64 = 65536 FLOP. Total per (i,j): ~77k FLOP. For 2048 positions, ~157M FLOP. Good. We can have each block handle one (b,i,j). Block size: maybe 64 or 128 threads. Each thread can compute a chunk of the output. Simplify: Use 64 threads per block. Each block computes one (b,i,j). Steps: 1. Load x_i = msa[b, :, i, :] (S*D = 8*64 = 512 floats) and x_j = msa[b, :, j, :] (512 floats) from global memory into shared memory. Actually we only need x_i for a and x_j for b. Wait, a uses x_i and b uses x_j. Because a = linear_a(x[b,s,i,:]), b = linear_b(x[b,s,j,:]). So we need x for position i across all s, and x for position j across all s. That's 2*S*D = 1024 floats = 4KB. Store in shared memory. But note: x is normalized. We need to compute LayerNorm per (b,s,n). We can compute LN on the fly for each (b,s,i) and (b,s,j). Since D=64, a warp can compute LN for one (b,s). We have S=8, so 8 warps. But we only have 64 threads (2 warps). So maybe each thread computes LN for multiple s. Let's break down per block of 64 threads: - Loop over s=0..7: - Load msa[b, s, i, 0..63] and msa[b, s, j, 0..63] into registers (or shared). Since D=64, 64 threads can load one float each for i and one for j. So for each s, we have a single warp (32 threads) or the whole block (64 threads) load the data. - Compute mean and variance over D=64. With 64 threads, each thread has one element. Use warp shuffle or shared memory reduction. We can do a tree reduction in shared memory. - Compute normalized x_i and x_j. - Compute a_s = W_a @ x_norm_i + b_a (size H=32). With 64 threads, each thread can compute one element of a_s (threads 0..31) and one element of b_s (threads 32..63) by dot product over D=64? Wait, each element of a_s is a dot product of length D. With 64 threads, we can have thread h compute a_s[h] by looping over d=0..63 and accumulating. But thread h only has one x_norm_i[d] loaded? Actually, after LN, x_norm_i is in shared memory (size D). Thread h loops over d and reads x_norm_i[d] and W_a[h,d] from global memory. This is 32*64 = 2048 reads per s for W_a. We can keep W_a in shared memory? W_a is (H,D) = 32*64 = 2048 floats = 8KB. W_b is another 8KB. Total 16KB. Plus x data. Shared memory limit 48KB, so we can fit W_a and W_b in shared memory! That would speed things up. Let's plan shared memory usage for OPM block: - x_i_buf: S*D = 512 floats - x_j_buf: S*D = 512 floats - a_buf: S*H = 256 floats (optional) - b_buf: S*H = 256 floats (optional) - outer_acc: H*H = 1024 floats - W_a: H*D = 2048 floats - W_b: H*D = 2048 floats - b_a: H = 32 floats - b_b: H = 32 floats - gamma_ln: D = 64 floats - beta_ln: D = 64 floats - W_out: P * (H*H) = 64*1024 = 65536 floats = 256KB. That's too big for shared memory. So W_out cannot fit. But we can compute the outer_acc vector and then compute the output using global memory for W_out. Shared memory total without W_out: 512+512+256+256+1024+2048+2048+32+32+64+64 = ~7.8KB. Very comfortable. We can even store x_i and x_j fully. So per block of 64 threads: 1. Load W_a, W_b into shared memory (if not cached). Or rely on L1 cache from global. Since all blocks read the same W_a, W_b, constant memory or __restrict__ with good caching works. But to be safe, we can load into shared memory. 2. For s=0..S-1: a. Load msa[b,s,i,:] into x_i_s[64] in shared mem (or registers). b. Compute LN: mean, var over 64 elements. With 64 threads, do a reduction to get mean, then variance, then normalize. Write normalized x back to shared mem. c. Thread h (0..31) computes a_s[h] = dot(x_i_norm, W_a[h,:]) + b_a[h]. Thread h can do this by keeping a running sum. Since x_i_norm is in shared mem and W_a is in shared mem (or global), each thread loops over D=64. d. Thread h (32..63) computes b_s[h-32] similarly using x_j_norm and W_b. e. Write a_s and b_s to shared mem a_buf[s,:] and b_buf[s,:]. 3. After all s, compute outer_acc: For each (h1, h2), sum_s a_buf[s,h1] * b_buf[s,h2]. We can have each thread compute a few elements of outer_acc. There are 1024 elements. With 64 threads, each thread computes 16 elements. Outer_acc is stored in shared mem. 4. Divide outer_acc by S. 5. Compute out = outer_acc @ W_out^T + b_out. Each thread computes a few output channels (P=64). With 64 threads, each thread computes 1 output channel. For channel p, thread loops over hh=0..1023, accumulates outer_acc[hh] * W_out[p, hh] + b_out[p]. W_out is in global memory. For coalesced access, W_out should be stored as [P, HH] row-major. Then thread p reads W_out[p, :] contiguously. Good. 6. Add residual: pair[b,i,j,p] += out[p]. This is very clean! Block size = 64 threads. Grid = (B, N, N) = 2048 blocks. That's great parallelism. S=8, D=64, H=32, HH=1024, P=64. Shared memory: x_i, x_j: 2*64 floats. Actually we can reuse the same shared mem arrays for each s, no need to store all s. a_buf and b_buf: we do need all s to compute outer. So a_buf = S*H = 8*32 = 256 floats. b_buf = 256. outer_acc = 1024. Total ~2KB. Plus W_a, W_b: 2048 each = 8KB. Total ~10KB. Well under 48KB. We also need gamma_ln and beta_ln for LayerNorm (2*64 = 128 floats). Fine. So OPM kernel is straightforward and highly parallel. #### TriMulOut Kernel Input: pair (B, N, N, P) (already updated with OPM residual) Output: pair += tri_mul_out. For each (b, i, j): 1. Load pair[b,i,j,:] (P=64) into registers. 2. Compute pa, ga, pb, gb for the i-row and j-row? Wait, TriMulOut uses pair in the einsum as well. Let's look again: ```python def forward(self, pair): x = self.norm(pair) a = self.pa(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.ga(x)) b = self.pb(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gb(x)) o = torch.einsum("bikh,bjkh->bijh", a, b) o = self.out(self.norm_out(o)) return torch.sigmoid(self.gate_out(pair)) * o ``` `pair` is the full (B,N,N,P) tensor. `x = self.norm(pair)` normalizes over P for each (b,i,j). `a = self.pa(x) * sigmoid(self.ga(x))` where `pa` and `ga` are linear maps from P to H_tri=32. `a` has shape (B,N,N,H_tri). Wait, a is computed from x which is (B,N,N,P). So a[b,i,k,h] = pa(x[b,i,k,:]) * sigmoid(ga(x[b,i,k,:])). Similarly b[b,j,k,h] = pb(x[b,j,k,:]) * sigmoid(gb(x[b,j,k,:])). Then o[b,i,j,h] = sum_k a[b,i,k,h] * b[b,j,k,h]. So for each (b,i,j,h), it's a dot product over k of a_{ik} and b_{jk}. This is exactly as described. Then `self.out(self.norm_out(o))`: o has shape (B,N,N,H_tri). Apply LayerNorm over H_tri, then linear H_tri->P. Then `torch.sigmoid(self.gate_out(pair)) * o`: gate is computed from original `pair` (not `x`? Wait, the code is `torch.sigmoid(self.gate_out(pair)) * o`. It uses `pair` (the input to the module, before norm). So gate uses the original pair representation (after OPM residual). So for our kernel 2b, the input is `pair` (after OPM addition). We need to compute gate from this pair directly (no norm). The code says `self.gate_out(pair)`, where pair is the argument to forward. So yes, the raw pair input to TriMulOut. Then multiply: sigmoid(gate_out(pair)) * out_linear. Finally, add residual: pair += result. So per (b, i, j) block, we need: - Compute gate = sigmoid(pair[b,i,j,:] @ W_gate^T + b_gate). This is P=64 in, P=64 out. Wait W_gate is (P,P). So gate is size P. But the final multiplication is gate * out, where out is P. So gate is per channel. `self.gate_out` is a Linear(P, P). So gate = sigmoid(Linear(pair_input)). That's P dot products of length P. P*P = 4096 FLOP. - Also we need to compute the einsum. For that, we need a_ik for all k and b_jk for all k. But a_ik depends on pair[b,i,k,:] (the i-row across k). Similarly b_jk depends on pair[b,j,k,:] (the j-row across k). So each block for (i,j) needs to load the entire i-row and j-row of pair. - i-row: pair[b, i, 0..N-1, :] = (N, P) = (32, 64). That's 2048 floats. - j-row: pair[b, j, 0..N-1, :] = (32, 64). Another 2048 floats. - Total 4096 floats = 16KB. This fits in shared memory. - For each k (0..31), we compute: - x_ik = LayerNorm(pair[b,i,k,:]) over P=64. - pa_ik = W_pa @ x_ik + b_pa (P->H, 64*32=2048 FLOP) - ga_ik = W_ga @ x_ik + b_ga - pb_jk = W_pb @ x_jk + b_pb - gb_jk = W_gb @ x_jk + b_gb - a_ik = pa_ik * sigmoid(ga_ik) (32 elementwise) - b_jk = pb_jk * sigmoid(gb_jk) Then for each h (0..31), accumulate o[h] += a_ik[h] * b_jk[h]. So per k, we do ~4 * (P*H) = 4*2048 = 8192 FLOP for linears, plus some elementwise. N=32, so total ~262k FLOP for einsum part. Plus gate 4096, plus out linear 32*64=2048, plus norm_out 64. So per (i,j) ~270k FLOP. For 2048 positions, ~550M FLOP. That's actually more than OPM. But OPM had a large linear (1024->64) per position, which was 134M total. TriMul einsum does many operations per position. Total FLOP for pair update: OPM 157M + TriMul 550M = ~707M. With 2048 blocks of 64 threads, each block does ~345k FLOP. At 1 TFLOP/s, that's 0.7 ms. Memory bandwidth for loading pair rows and msa positions is also a factor. This is very parallel and should be fast. So we design kernel 2b similarly. Block size: maybe 64 or 128 threads. If block size = 64 threads: - We need to compute a_ik and b_jk for all k=0..31. Each k requires loading pair[b,i,k,:] and pair[b,j,k,:] into shared memory. We can load the entire i-row and j-row into shared memory upfront. - i_row_shared: N * P = 2048 floats. - j_row_shared: 2048 floats. - We also need W_pa, W_ga, W_pb, W_gb. Each is P*H = 64*32 = 2048 floats = 8KB. Four of them = 32KB. Plus b biases: 4*32 = 128 floats. - We need norm_out gamma/beta: H=32 floats each. - W_out_tri: H*P = 32*64 = 2048 floats = 8KB. - W_gate: P*P = 4096 floats = 16KB. - Additionally, we need gamma/beta for the input LN of pair: P=64 each. Total shared memory for weights and data: i_row + j_row = 8KB. W's = 32KB + 8KB + 16KB = 56KB. Plus overhead, this exceeds 48KB limit. So we cannot put all weight matrices in shared memory. We need to be selective. Since P=64 and H=32, each weight matrix is 8KB. We could put two of them in shared memory at a time, or just read from global memory with caching. Global memory bandwidth is 1-2 TB/s, and these weights are small. Each block reads W_pa (8KB), W_ga (8KB), W_pb (8KB), W_gb (8KB), W_out_tri (8KB), W_gate (16KB). Total per block = 56KB of weight reads. 2048 blocks -> total weight reads = 112MB. Since weights are shared across all blocks, they will be in L2 cache. L2 cache size is many MBs (e.g., 40MB on A100). So L2 will cache them perfectly. So reading from global memory is fine. Thus, we can skip loading weight matrices into shared memory and rely on L2 cache. For the pair rows, reading from global memory into shared memory is good for reuse. Shared memory for kernel 2b: - i_row: N*P = 2048 floats - j_row: 2048 floats - o_accum: H = 32 floats - gate: P = 64 floats - out_buf: P = 64 floats - x_ik: P = 64 floats (reused per k) - x_jk: P = 64 floats - pa, ga, pb, gb: each H=32, total 128 floats - Temporary for reductions. Total ~ (2048+2048+32+64+64+64+64+128) floats ~ 4512 floats = ~18KB. That's well within 48KB. So kernel 2b with 64 threads is feasible. Algorithm per block (64 threads): 1. Determine b, i, j from block indices. 2. Load pair[b, i, k, :] for all k into i_row_shared. Use 64 threads to load: total N*P = 2048 floats. Each thread loads 32 floats. Similarly load pair[b, j, k, :] into j_row_shared. 3. Compute gate from pair_input[b,i,j,:]: - Thread p (0..63) computes dot(pair_input[b,i,j,:], W_gate[p,:]) + b_gate[p]. This requires reading pair_input (loaded into register or shared) and W_gate from global. We can have each thread compute its gate value and store in gate_shared[p]. - Apply sigmoid. (Or we can compute gate later after we compute the einsum part. But it doesn't depend on einsum, so can be done anytime.) 4. Compute o_accum[h] = 0 for h=0..31. Since we have 64 threads, threads 0..31 can hold o_accum in registers. Or store in shared and initialize. 5. For k = 0 .. N-1: a. Load x_ik = i_row_shared[k, :] (P=64) into a local array (register). But with 64 threads, we can have thread p load x_ik[p] into a register. b. Compute LN on x_ik: mean and variance over p. Use warp/block reduction. Write normalized x_ik_norm to shared mem x_ik_shared (64 floats). (We can reuse the same shared buffer). c. Compute pa and ga: - Thread h (0..31) computes pa[h] = dot(x_ik_norm, W_pa[h,:]) + b_pa[h]. Since x_ik_norm is in shared, W_pa in global. - Thread h similarly computes ga[h]. - Similarly threads 32..63 compute pb and gb for x_jk (j_row_shared[k,:]). d. Compute a_ik[h] = pa[h] * sigmoid(ga[h]). b_jk similarly. e. Thread h (0..31) accumulates o_accum[h] += a_ik[h] * b_jk[h] (note b_jk is computed by threads 32..63, need to broadcast or store to shared). After computing pb and gb, write b_jk to shared b_shared[32]. Then thread h reads b_shared[h] and multiplies. Or we can have threads 32..63 do nothing in this step? Better: after step c, write a_ik and b_jk to shared mem (a_shared[32], b_shared[32]). Then all threads (or threads 0..31) read a_shared[h] and b_shared[h] and multiply-accumulate. f. __syncthreads(). 6. After loop k, we have o_accum (size 32). Apply LayerNorm to o_accum: - Compute mean and variance over h=0..31 (32 elements). Use block reduction. - Normalize. 7. Compute out_linear = o_accum @ W_out_tri^T + b_out_tri. - Thread p (0..63) computes dot(o_accum_norm, W_out_tri[p,:]) + b_out_tri[p]. W_out_tri is shape (P, H) = (64, 32). - Store in out_shared[p]. 8. Multiply with gate: final_p = gate_shared[p] * out_shared[p]. 9. Add residual: pair[b,i,j,p] += final_p. Wait: the residual add is `pair_repr = pair_repr + self.tri_mul(pair_repr)`. So pair_output = pair_input + tri_mul_out. Our block loaded pair_input into i_row_shared and j_row_shared. But pair_input for (i,j) is also needed for gate and the residual add. We can keep pair_ij in registers: - At step 1, while loading i_row_shared, thread p also loads pair_input[b,i,j,p] into register pair_ij_p. - In step 3, compute gate from pair_ij_p. - In step 9, pair_output = pair_ij_p + final_p. Write back to global pair[b,i,j,p]. So we don't need a separate buffer for pair_input. One subtlety: In step 5a, x_ik is i_row_shared[k,p]. Since i_row_shared is in shared memory, loading is fast. Similarly for j. Another subtlety: LayerNorm on pair_input for each (i,k) and (j,k). We need gamma/beta for pair LN (TriMulOut's `self.norm`). That's P=64. What about the sigmoid on gate? `torch.sigmoid(self.gate_out(pair))`. gate_out is a linear on pair_input (not normalized). Wait, `self.gate_out(pair)` where pair is the argument to forward. So yes, pair_input is used directly. We have pair_ij_p. Good. Also note: `o = self.out(self.norm_out(o))`. `self.norm_out` is LayerNorm on hidden=H_tri. So we need gamma_norm_out (H_tri) and beta_norm_out (H_tri). Then `self.out` is Linear(H_tri, pair_dim). Everything fits. Now, how many FLOPs per block? - Loading pair rows: negligible. - Gate: P*P = 4096 FLOP. - For each k=32: - 2 LNs: each reduces over P=64, then elementwise mul. Negligible vs linears. - 4 linears: 4 * P*H = 4 * 64*32 = 8192 FLOP. - 2 sigmoids + 2 elementwise muls: negligible. - Multiply-accumulate into o_accum: H = 32 FLOP. Total per k: ~8224 FLOP. For 32 k: ~263k. - LN on o_accum: negligible. - out linear: P*H = 2048 FLOP. - elementwise mul with gate: P=64. Total ~270k FLOP per block. 2048 blocks -> 550M FLOP. At 1 TFLOP/s, that's 0.55 ms. Memory reads: i_row + j_row = 8KB per block. 2048 blocks = 16MB. But these are cached across blocks because different (i,j) blocks read overlapping rows. L2 cache will reduce actual DRAM traffic. This seems very efficient. #### MSA Attention Kernel (Kernel 1) Now, how to handle MSA attention? We need to be careful. Writing a full custom attention kernel from scratch is complex but we can do a simple one. Since B=2,S=8,N=32,H=4,d=16,D=64. Total blocks if we do one per (b,s,h,block_i?). Actually, we can have each block handle one (b,s) and all heads, using shared memory as described earlier. That gives 16 blocks, which is low. But we can also have each block handle a subset of positions n. Or we can have a 2D grid: blocks over (b,s,h) with each block handling N query positions? Let's explore. Idea: block per (b,s,h). Grid = B*S*H = 64 blocks. Each block size: 32 threads (one per query row). Each thread computes Q_i, K_i? Wait, each block needs Q and K for all positions to compute QK^T. But Q and K are computed from x in global memory. We can precompute Q, K, V in a separate simple kernel, or compute them on the fly. Let me analyze the model structure and plan a fused CUDA kernel. The model is: 1. MSARowSelfAttention(msa_dim=64, num_heads=4) - LayerNorm(msa_dim) - Linear(msa_dim->msa_dim, bias=False) for q, k, v - reshape q,k,v: (B,S,N,h,d) -> permute(0,1,3,2,4): (B,S,h,N,d) - q @ k.T / sqrt(d) -> (B,S,h,N,N) - softmax - (attn @ v) -> permute(0,1,3,2,4) -> reshape -> (B,S,N,D) - Linear(D->D) - Residual: msa = msa + output 2. OuterProductMeanSmall(msa_dim=64, pair_dim=64, hidden=32) - LayerNorm(msa_dim) - Linear(msa_dim->hidden) for a, b - einsum("bsih,bsjk->bijhk", a, b) - divide by S - Linear(hidden*hidden -> pair_dim) - Residual: pair = pair + output 3. TriMulOut(pair_dim=64, hidden=32) - LayerNorm(pair_dim) - Linear(pair_dim->hidden) for pa, pb, ga, gb - a = pa * sigmoid(ga), b = pb * sigmoid(gb) - einsum("bikh,bjkh->bijh", a, b) - LayerNorm(hidden) + Linear(hidden -> pair_dim) - out2 = sigmoid(gate_out(pair)) * out - Residual: pair = pair + out2 So operations for MSA: - Input msa: (B, S, N, D=64) - MSA attention with small dimensions - OPM: reduces MSA along S dimension to produce pair update For Pair: - OPM adds to pair - TriMulOut operates on pair We can potentially fuse the entire forward pass into one or a few kernels. So the mapping I described is actually great for coalescing: Within a warp, threads handle the same n and adjacent d_out. Given the complexity, let me write 3 reasonably optimized kernels but keeping them readable. For Kernel 1 (MSA attention): ```cuda __global__ void msa_attn_forward( const float* __restrict__ msa, float* __restrict__ out_msa, int B, int S, int N, int D, int H, int d, const float* __restrict__ norm_w, const float* __restrict__ norm_b, const float* __restrict__ qw, const float* __restrict__ kw, const float* __restrict__ vw, const float* __restrict__ ow, const float* __restrict__ ob) { int b = blockIdx.x; int s = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Shared memory // smem[0 : N*D] = msa // smem[N*D : 2*N*D] = qkv buffer / attn output // smem[2*N*D : 2*N*D + H*N*N] = attention scores (if needed) // However, to keep it simple, we'll store intermediate attention results // in registers and shared memory distributed per warp/head. // Actually let me use a simpler approach: one block per (b,s,h) for QKV and attention, // then a reduction-like kernel or second phase for output linear. // BUT that increases kernel launches. Let's do one block per (b,s). } ``` Hmm, one block per (b,s,h) might actually be cleaner and gives us more parallelism: - Blocks: B * S * H = 2 * 8 * 4 = 64 blocks. - Each block has 32 threads (one warp). - Block (b,s,h) computes: 1. Load msa[b,s,:,:] into shared memory (all warps within block cooperate... wait, there's only one warp per block). 2. Compute q, k, v for head h. 3. Compute attention for head h. 4. Write result to a global scratch buffer of shape (B, S, H, N, d). Then we need a second kernel to combine heads and do output linear + residual. This might be easier to write correctly. Two kernels for MSA attention: 1. `msa_attn_heads`: per (b,s,h) block, computes one head's attention. - Each block (one warp = 32 threads): a. Load msa row for its N index... actually all threads need all msa. b. Load msa into registers or smem. smem: N*D = 8KB. c. LayerNorm per row (need to compute). But all blocks for same (b,s) would redundantly compute LayerNorm! d. To avoid redundant LayerNorm, we can precompute it in a separate kernel, OR accept redundancy (64 blocks * 32 rows of LayerNorm). The LayerNorm compute is small: 64 * 32 * 64 = 131K ops. Negligible. Actually, the best approach for correctness and speed: accept that I need to carefully implement one block per (b,s) and do it right. OK I think I should use the warp-per-head approach within one block per (b,s), and write it carefully. Let me draft the kernel with 128 threads (4 warps): ```cuda __global__ void msa_attn_kernel( const float* __restrict__ msa, float* __restrict__ out_msa, int B, int S, int N, int D, int num_heads, int head_dim, const float* __restrict__ norm_w, const float* __restrict__ norm_b, const float* __restrict__ qw, const float* __restrict__ kw, const float* __restrict__ vw, const float* __restrict__ ow, const float* __restrict__ ob) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; // Layout: // [0, N*D) : smem_msa // [N*D, 2*N*D) : smem_attn (attention output before linear) float* smem_msa = smem; float* smem_attn = smem + N * D; int b = blockIdx.x; int s = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane = tid % 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; // Step 1: Load msa into shared memory (all threads) const float* msa_ptr = msa + ((b * S + s) * N) * D; for (int i = tid; i < N * D; i += blockDim.x) { smem_msa[i] = msa_ptr[i]; } __syncthreads(); // Step 2: LayerNorm per row // Each warp handles ceil(N / num_warps) rows for (int n = warp_id; n < N; n += num_warps) { // Mean float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = lane; d < D; d += 32) { sum += smem_msa[n * D + d]; } // Warp reduce sum #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum, offset); } float mean = sum / D; // Var float var_sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = lane; d < D; d += 32) { float diff = smem_msa[n * D + d] - mean; var_sum += diff * diff; } #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { var_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, var_sum, offset); } float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f); // Normalize and write back #pragma unroll for (int d = lane; d < D; d += 32) { int idx = n * D + d; smem_msa[idx] = (smem_msa[idx] - mean) * inv_std * norm_w[d] + norm_b[d]; } } __syncthreads(); // Step 3-7: Each warp computes one head's attention // Q, K, V are stored in registers per lane // K is also stored in smem for cross-thread access // We need per-warp shared memory for K and V (32 * head_dim each) // Allocate per-warp smem dynamically or use static partitioning // For simplicity, declare K and V in larger smem // smem_k: [2*N*D, 2*N*D + num_warps*N*head_dim) // smem_v: [2*N*D + num_warps*N*head_dim, ...) float* smem_k = smem + 2 * N * D + warp_id * N * head_dim; float* smem_v = smem + 2 * N * D + num_warps * N * head_dim + warp_id * N * head_dim; // Wait, we need enough shared memory. Let's calculate: // smem_msa: N*D = 2048 // smem_attn: N*D = 2048 // smem_k: 4 * 32 * 16 = 2048 // smem_v: 4 * 32 * 16 = 2048 // Total: 8192 floats = 32KB. // We need to declare this in the launch or use dynamic. // Actually, we can compute K and V and store them directly in smem_k and smem_v // while keeping Q in registers. // For head h: int h = warp_id; if (h < num_heads) { // Compute Q[n,:], K[n,:], V[n,:] for this lane's n // Each lane handles one n (0..31) int n = lane; float q_vec[16]; float v_vec[16]; // Compute Q for (int d_out = 0; d_out < head_dim; d_out++) { float sum = 0.0f; const float* w_row = qw + (h * head_dim + d_out) * D; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; d_in++) { sum += smem_msa[n * D + d_in] * w_row[d_in]; } q_vec[d_out] = sum; } // Compute K and store to smem_k for (int d_out = 0; d_out < head_dim; d_out++) { float sum = 0.0f; const float* w_row = kw + (h * head_dim + d_out) * D; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; d_in++) { sum += smem_msa[n * D + d_in] * w_row[d_in]; } smem_k[n * head_dim + d_out] = sum; } // Compute V and store to smem_v for (int d_out = 0; d_out < head_dim; d_out++) { float sum = 0.0f; const float* w_row = vw + (h * head_dim + d_out) * D; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; d_in++) { sum += smem_msa[n * D + d_in] * w_row[d_in]; } v_vec[d_out] = sum; smem_v[n * head_dim + d_out] = sum; } __syncwarp(); // Compute attention scores float scores[32]; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int d_out = 0; d_out < head_dim; d_out++) { dot += q_vec[d_out] * smem_k[m * head_dim + d_out]; } scores[m] = dot * scale; } // Softmax float max_score = scores[0]; for (int m = 1; m < N; m++) { max_score = fmaxf(max_score, scores[m]); } float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { scores[m] = expf(scores[m] - max_score); exp_sum += scores[m]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / exp_sum; for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { scores[m] *= inv_sum; } // Attn @ V float o_vec[16]; for (int d_out = 0; d_out < head_dim; d_out++) { o_vec[d_out] = 0.0f; } for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { float s = scores[m]; for (int d_out = 0; d_out < head_dim; d_out++) { o_vec[d_out] += s * smem_v[m * head_dim + d_out]; } } // Write to smem_attn for (int d_out = 0; d_out < head_dim; d_out++) { smem_attn[n * D + h * head_dim + d_out] = o_vec[d_out]; } } __syncthreads(); // Step 8: Output linear and residual // Each thread handles multiple output elements for (int i = tid; i < N * D; i += blockDim.x) { int n = i / D; int d_out = i % D; float sum = ob[d_out]; const float* w_row = ow + d_out * D; for (int d_in = 0; d_in < D; d_in++) { sum += smem_attn[n * D + d_in] * w_row[d_in]; } out_msa[((b * S + s) * N + n) * D + d_out] = msa_ptr[i] + sum; } } ``` Wait, there are some issues: 1. I used `__syncwarp()` for warp synchronization. But I need to make sure only threads in the warp call it. With `if (h < num_heads)`, threads with warp_id >= num_heads skip it. If num_heads=4 and num_warps=4 (128 threads), this is fine. But if I use 256 threads, warps 4-7 would skip it and deadlock. Fix: use 128 threads (4 warps). 2. Shared memory for smem_k and smem_v: I need to declare it properly. In my code, I defined pointers based on smem base, but I need to make sure the kernel launch requests enough shared memory. For simplicity, I can define pointers relative to a larger smem array. 3. The LayerNorm loop: `for (int n = warp_id; n < N; n += num_warps)`. With 4 warps and N=32, each warp handles 8 rows. Within a warp, the reduction with __shfl_down only works across the warp. Thread 0 handles row 0, thread 1 handles row 1, etc. The __shfl_down will mix values from different rows! Fix: Each thread in warp handles one row, but __shfl_down reduces across all lanes. If lane n handles row n, then __shfl_down will get values from other rows, which is WRONG. Actually, __shfl_down gets values from other lanes in the SAME warp. So if lane 0 computes sum for row 0, lane 1 computes sum for row 1, then when doing __shfl_down(mask, sum, 16), lane 0 gets lane 16's sum (which is for row 16). That's exactly what we want! We want to sum all lanes' sums to get total sum across all rows... wait no. For a single row n, only lane n is computing the sum. Other lanes are computing sums for OTHER rows. So __shfl_down across the warp would sum lane 0 (row 0) + lane 16 (row 16). That's summing DIFFERENT rows, not the same row. I need all threads in the warp to work on the SAME row. So for row 0: threads 0-31 all compute partial sums of row 0. Then __shfl_down gives total sum of row 0. Fix: LayerNorm should be done differently. Option A: Each thread handles one row. Compute mean by iterating over all D elements. No shuffle needed. D=64, so each thread does 64 loads and adds. Easy. Option B: Have the warp process one row together. Since D=64 is small, Option A is fine. Each of 32 threads handles one row. 32 threads work, rest idle. But with 4 warps, we can have 128 threads. Simpler: 64 threads handle one row (but we only have 64 elements). Actually for D=64, we can have 64 threads per row. But 32 rows * 64 threads = 2048 threads, way too many. Let's do: thread tid handle rows tid, tid+32, tid+64, etc. but only up to N=32. So threads 0..31 each handle one row. Threads 32..127 idle for LayerNorm. For row n (lane = n): ``` float sum = 0; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) sum += smem_msa[n*D + d]; mean = sum / D; float var_sum = 0; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float diff = smem_msa[n*D+d] - mean; var_sum += diff*diff; } inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f); for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) smem_msa[n*D+d] = (smem_msa[n*D+d] - mean) * inv_std * norm_w[d] + norm_b[d]; ``` Each thread does 3*64 = 192 ops. 32 threads = 6K ops. Negligible. Let me fix the LayerNorm. 4. MSA attention output: I write to smem_attn using warp-per-head, where lane n writes to row n. This is correct. 5. The msa_ptr loading and later residual addition: I store msa_ptr at the start, but then the pointer is relative to (b,s). When adding residual, I use msa_ptr[i] directly. But msa_ptr was set to `msa + ((b * S + s) * N) * D`. So msa_ptr[i] is the original msa value. Good. 6. PyTorch linear weights: `nn.Linear` weights are (out_features, in_features). In CUDA, qw[d_out * in_features + d_in]. My code uses `qw + (h * head_dim + d_out) * D + d_in`. That's correct. 7. For the output linear: `ow + d_out * D + d_in`. That's correct. 8. Quantization: PyTorch linear with bias=False doesn't have bias. But MSA attention's `self.out` has bias=True (default). So we add ob. Let me fix LayerNorm and resubmit the kernel concept. Actually wait, I said threads 32-127 idle for LayerNorm. That's fine but wastes 75% of threads. We can optimize by having more threads participate: e.g., thread tid handles row tid % 32, and within a row, each thread handles a subset of elements for mean/var. But with D=64 and 128 threads, we'd have 4 threads per row. Each computes partial sum of 16 elements. Then need to reduce across 4 threads in different warps... which is harder. Given the tiny compute of LayerNorm, I'll just use 32 threads and __syncthreads after. Let me rewrite: ```cuda __global__ void msa_attn_kernel(...) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_msa = smem; float* smem_attn = smem + N * D; float* smem_k = smem + 2 * N * D; // size: num_warps * N * head_dim float* smem_v = smem + 2 * N * D + num_warps * N * head_dim; // same size int b = blockIdx.x; int s = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int warp_id = tid / 32; int lane = tid % 32; int num_warps = blockDim.x / 32; const float* msa_ptr = msa + ((b * S + s) * N) * D; // Load msa for (int i = tid; i < N * D; i += blockDim.x) { smem_msa[i] = msa_ptr[i]; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm: 32 threads handle 32 rows if (tid < 32) { int n = tid; float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) sum += smem_msa[n * D + d]; float mean = sum / D; float var_sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float diff = smem_msa[n * D + d] - mean; var_sum += diff * diff; } float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f); for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { smem_msa[n * D + d] = (smem_msa[n * D + d] - mean) * inv_std * norm_w[d] + norm_b[d]; } } __syncthreads(); // Per-head attention int h = warp_id; if (h < num_heads) { int n = lane; float q_vec[16]; float scores[32]; float o_vec[16]; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); float* my_k = smem_k + warp_id * N * head_dim; float* my_v = smem_v + warp_id * N * head_dim; // Q for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) { float s = 0.0f; const float* w = qw + (h * head_dim + dh) * D; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { s += smem_msa[n * D + d] * w[d]; } q_vec[dh] = s; } // K for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) { float s = 0.0f; const float* w = kw + (h * head_dim + dh) * D; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { s += smem_msa[n * D + d] * w[d]; } my_k[n * head_dim + dh] = s; } // V for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) { float s = 0.0f; const float* w = vw + (h * head_dim + dh) * D; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { s += smem_msa[n * D + d] * w[d]; } my_v[n * head_dim + dh] = s; } __syncwarp(); // Scores for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { float d = 0.0f; for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) { d += q_vec[dh] * my_k[m * head_dim + dh]; } scores[m] = d * scale; } // Softmax float max_s = scores[0]; for (int m = 1; m < N; m++) max_s = fmaxf(max_s, scores[m]); float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { scores[m] = expf(scores[m] - max_s); exp_sum += scores[m]; } float inv_es = 1.0f / exp_sum; for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) scores[m] *= inv_es; // Attn @ V for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) o_vec[dh] = 0.0f; for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { float s = scores[m]; for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) { o_vec[dh] += s * my_v[m * head_dim + dh]; } } // Write to smem_attn for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) { smem_attn[n * D + h * head_dim + dh] = o_vec[dh]; } } __syncthreads(); // Output linear + residual for (int i = tid; i < N * D; i += blockDim.x) { int n = i / D; int d_out = i % D; float s = ob[d_out]; const float* w = ow + d_out * D; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { s += smem_attn[n * D + d] * w[d]; } out_msa[((b * S + s) * N + n) * D + d_out] = msa_ptr[i] + s; } } ``` Wait, for the output linear thread loop: `i` ranges from tid to N*D with step blockDim.x. If blockDim.x = 128 and N*D = 2048, each thread handles 16 elements. `n = i / D` (64) and `d_out = i % D`. For tid=0: i=0, n=0, d=0; i=128, n=2, d=0; i=256, n=4, d=0... This strided access is not coalesced for writes. To make it coalesced: have threads in a warp handle adjacent d_out for the same n. Better mapping: For each output position (n, d_out): total 2048. Block of 128 threads. Thread tid assigned to positions starting at tid * 16, of length 16. This gives contiguous positions per thread. tid 0: (0, 0..15) tid 1: (0, 16..31) tid 2: (0, 32..47) tid 3: (0, 48..63) tid 4: (1, 0..15) ... tid 127: (31, 48..63) Within a warp (32 threads): tid 0-3: n=0, d=0..15, 16..31, 32..47, 48..63 tid 4-7: n=1, ... ... tid 28-31: n=7, ... These threads write to adjacent memory? Thread 0 writes to [0*64 + 0], thread 1 writes to [0*64 + 16]. Not perfectly adjacent but within the same cache line. OK. Actually for writes to be coalesced, threads in a warp should write to adjacent addresses. If all threads handle the same n but different d_out: thread 0 writes address base+0, thread1 writes base+1, etc. That's perfect coalescing. With my mapping, threads handle different n and different d_out. tid0: n=0,d=0..15 -> writes 16 floats starting at addr 0. tid1: n=0,d=16..31 -> writes 16 floats starting at addr 16. tid2: n=0,d=32..47 -> writes starting at addr 32. tid3: n=0,d=48..63 -> writes starting at addr 48. tid4: n=1,d=0..15 -> writes starting at addr 64. These are all adjacent! tid0 writes [0,15], tid1 writes [16,31], tid2 writes [32,47], tid3 writes [48,63], tid4 writes [64,79]. So writes are perfectly coalesced within the warp. Great. For reads during the dot product: each thread reads w[d_out, :] which is row d_out of ow. Adjacent threads read different rows. Memory access is strided by D=64 floats = 256 bytes. That's not perfectly coalesced but within L1 cache it should be fine. And each thread reads smem_attn[n, :] which is row n. Adjacent threads (tid0-3) read n=0 repeatedly. That's broadcast. Fine. This looks good. Since pair is read from global memory, and we're doing many random-ish accesses (per i,j we read different slices), L2 cache might help a lot. Actually, pair layout is [B, N, N, D_pair]. For thread block (b,i,j), the reads are: - pair[b, i, 0, :] at offset (b*N*N + i*N + 0)*D_pair - pair[b, i, 1, :] at offset + D_pair - ... - pair[b, j, 0, :] at offset (b*N*N + j*N + 0)*D_pair These accesses are strided but contiguous within each row. Let's structure the TriMul kernel with block per (b, i, j): Actually, I just realized that for TriMulOut, the pair is updated as `pair = pair + output`. So we need to read the original pair, compute output, then write back `pair + output`. Since the output depends on pair, we need to read pair before writing. Also: the `gate_out` uses the ORIGINAL pair (after norm). Wait: ```python x = self.norm(pair) a = self.pa(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.ga(x)) b = self.pb(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gb(x)) o = torch.einsum("bikh,bjkh->bijh", a, b) o = self.out(self.norm_out(o)) return torch.sigmoid(self.gate_out(pair)) * o ``` Note: `gate_out(pair)` uses `pair` directly, NOT `self.norm(pair)`. The code uses `pair`, not `x`. So for TriMul: 1. x = LayerNorm(pair) 2. From x (normed pair), compute a, b 3. o = einsum(a, b) 4. o = LayerNorm(o) -> Linear -> out 5. gate = sigmoid(Linear(pair) + bias) 6. result = gate * o 7. pair = pair + result Now, `pair` in gate is the original pair, not the normed one. For block (b, i, j): - Load pair[b, i, k, :] for all k = 0..N-1 - Load pair[b, j, k, :] for all k = 0..N-1 - Load pair[b, i, j, :] for gate - LayerNorm each row of pair[b, i, k, :] and pair[b, j, k, :] - Compute a[k, h] = pa(x_i[k]) * sigmoid(ga(x_i[k])) - Compute b[k, h] = pb(x_j[k]) * sigmoid(gb(x_j[k])) - o[h] = sum_k a[k,h] * b[k,h] - out_linear(norm_out(o)) -> out_vec[64] - gate_vec = sigmoid(gate_linear(pair[b,i,j,:]) + bias) - result = gate_vec * out_vec - pair[b,i,j,:] += result Wait, there's also `self.norm_out(o)` before `self.out(o)`. And `self.out` is `nn.Linear(hidden, pair_dim)`. So steps for o: - o is (hidden,). Apply LayerNorm: `norm_out_w` and `norm_out_b`. - Then Linear: `out_w` (pair_dim, hidden), `out_b` (pair_dim,). For one block (b, i, j): - Reads: pair_i_k (N * D_pair), pair_j_k (N * D_pair), pair_ij (D_pair) - Computes: - 2*N LayerNorms - 4*N linear projections (64->32): pa, ga, pb, gb - 2*N sigmoid + elementwise mul - N*H dot products for einsum - 1 LayerNorm on H - 1 Linear H->64 - 1 Linear 64->64 + sigmoid for gate - elementwise mul + add - Total ops: ~20K - Memory: read ~17KB, write ~256B 2048 blocks * 20K ops = 40M ops. 40M / 67T = 0.6 microseconds compute. Memory: 2048 * 17KB = 34MB read. 34MB / 3.35TB/s = 10 microseconds. Clearly memory bound. To optimize memory, we should maximize cache hits. When block (b,i,j) reads pair[b,i,0:N,:], and block (b,i,j+1) also reads pair[b,i,0:N,:], these are repeated reads. L2 cache should capture this if blocks run close in time. Similarly for pair[b,j,0:N,:]. Alternatively, block per (b, i) computing all j=0..31: - Block (b,i): - Load pair[b,i,0:N,:] once. - For each j=0..31: - Load pair[b,j,0:N,:] - Compute all steps - Write result - Shared memory for pair_i: N*D_pair = 2KB. - For j's data: N*D_pair = 2KB. - Total small. Shared memory for OPM block: - smem_msa_i: S * D_msa = 512 floats - smem_msa_j: S * D_msa = 512 floats - smem_a: S * H = 256 floats - smem_b: S * H = 256 floats - smem_outer: H * H = 1024 floats Total: ~12KB. For OPM block (b,i,j): ```cuda __global__ void opm_kernel( const float* msa, float* pair, int B, int S, int N, int D_msa, int H, int D_pair, const float* norm_w, const float* norm_b, const float* aw, const float* ab, const float* bw, const float* bb, const float* ow, const float* ob) { // Each block handles (b, i, j) int b = blockIdx.x; int i = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* smem_i = smem; float* smem_j = smem + S * D_msa; float* smem_a = smem + 2 * S * D_msa; float* smem_b = smem + 2 * S * D_msa + S * H; float* smem_outer = smem + 2 * S * D_msa + 2 * S * H; // Load msa[b, :, i, :] and msa[b, :, j, :] for (int idx = tid; idx < S * D_msa; idx += blockDim.x) { int s = idx / D_msa; int d = idx % D_msa; smem_i[idx] = msa[(((b * S + s) * N + i) * D_msa) + d]; smem_j[idx] = msa[(((b * S + s) * N + j) * D_msa) + d]; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm for S*2 rows // S=8, so 16 rows total. // Use 16 threads if blockDim.x >= 16, or all threads in a loop. for (int row = tid; row < 2 * S; row += blockDim.x) { float* row_ptr = (row < S) ? smem_i + row * D_msa : smem_j + (row - S) * D_msa; float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D_msa; d++) sum += row_ptr[d]; float mean = sum / D_msa; float var = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D_msa; d++) { float diff = row_ptr[d] - mean; var += diff * diff; } float inv_std = rsqrtf(var / D_msa + 1e-5f); for (int d = 0; d < D_msa; d++) { row_ptr[d] = (row_ptr[d] - mean) * inv_std * norm_w[d] + norm_b[d]; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute a and b for (int idx = tid; idx < S * H; idx += blockDim.x) { int s = idx / H; int h = idx % H; float a_val = ab[h]; float b_val = bb[h]; for (int d = 0; d < D_msa; d++) { a_val += smem_i[s * D_msa + d] * aw[h * D_msa + d]; b_val += smem_j[s * D_msa + d] * bw[h * D_msa + d]; } smem_a[s * H + h] = a_val; smem_b[s * H + h] = b_val; } __syncthreads(); // Compute outer product and sum over s for (int idx = tid; idx < H * H; idx += blockDim.x) { int h1 = idx / H; int h2 = idx % H; float sum = 0.0f; for (int s = 0; s < S; s++) { sum += smem_a[s * H + h1] * smem_b[s * H + h2]; } smem_outer[idx] = sum / (float)S; } __syncthreads(); // Linear projection and residual add to pair float* pair_ptr = pair + (((b * N + i) * N + j) * D_pair); for (int d_out = tid; d_out < D_pair; d_out += blockDim.x) { float sum = ob[d_out]; for (int h = 0; h < H * H; h++) { sum += smem_outer[h] * ow[d_out * (H * H) + h]; } pair_ptr[d_out] += sum; } } ``` Wait, there's an issue: `ow` is from `nn.Linear(hidden*hidden, pair_dim)`. Weight shape: (pair_dim, hidden*hidden). Access: `ow[d_out * (H*H) + h]`. This is correct for row-major. For TriMul kernel: ```cuda __global__ void trimul_kernel( const float* pair, float* out_pair, int B, int N, int D_pair, int H, const float* norm_w, const float* norm_b, const float* pa_w, const float* pa_b, const float* ga_w, const float* ga_b, const float* pb_w, const float* pb_b, const float* gb_w, const float* gb_b, const float* norm_out_w, const float* norm_out_b, const float* out_w, const float* out_b, const float* gate_w, const float* gate_b) { // Block per (b, i, j) int b = blockIdx.x; int i = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; extern __shared__ float smem[]; // smem layout: // x_i: N * D_pair // x_j: N * D_pair // a: N * H // b: N * H // o: H // out: D_pair float* smem_xi = smem; float* smem_xj = smem + N * D_pair; float* smem_a = smem + 2 * N * D_pair; float* smem_b = smem + 2 * N * D_pair + N * H; float* smem_o = smem + 2 * N * D_pair + 2 * N * H; float* smem_out = smem + 2 * N * D_pair + 2 * N * H + H; // Load pair[b, i, k, :] for all k and pair[b, j, k, :] for all k const float* pair_i_base = pair + ((b * N + i) * N) * D_pair; const float* pair_j_base = pair + ((b * N + j) * N) * D_pair; for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D_pair; idx += blockDim.x) { smem_xi[idx] = pair_i_base[idx]; smem_xj[idx] = pair_j_base[idx]; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm on xi and xj rows for (int row = tid; row < 2 * N; row += blockDim.x) { float* row_ptr = (row < N) ? smem_xi + row * D_pair : smem_xj + (row - N) * D_pair; float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D_pair; d++) sum += row_ptr[d]; float mean = sum / D_pair; float var = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D_pair; d++) { float diff = row_ptr[d] - mean; var += diff * diff; } float inv_std = rsqrtf(var / D_pair + 1e-5f); for (int d = 0; d < D_pair; d++) { row_ptr[d] = (row_ptr[d] - mean) * inv_std * norm_w[d] + norm_b[d]; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute a[k,h] = pa(x_i[k]) * sigmoid(ga(x_i[k])) for (int idx = tid; idx < N * H; idx += blockDim.x) { int k = idx / H; int h = idx % H; float pa = pa_b[h]; float ga = ga_b[h]; for (int d = 0; d < D_pair; d++) { pa += smem_xi[k * D_pair + d] * pa_w[h * D_pair + d]; ga += smem_xi[k * D_pair + d] * ga_w[h * D_pair + d]; } smem_a[idx] = pa * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-ga))); } __syncthreads(); // Compute b[k,h] = pb(x_j[k]) * sigmoid(gb(x_j[k])) for (int idx = tid; idx < N * H; idx += blockDim.x) { int k = idx / H; int h = idx % H; float pb = pb_b[h]; float gb = gb_b[h]; for (int d = 0; d < D_pair; d++) { pb += smem_xj[k * D_pair + d] * pb_w[h * D_pair + d]; gb += smem_xj[k * D_pair + d] * gb_w[h * D_pair + d]; } smem_b[idx] = pb * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gb))); } __syncthreads(); // Compute o[h] = sum_k a[k,h] * b[k,h] if (tid < H) { int h = tid; float sum = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < N; k++) { sum += smem_a[k * H + h] * smem_b[k * H + h]; } smem_o[h] = sum; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm on o if (tid < H) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) sum += smem_o[h]; float mean = sum / H; float var = 0.0f; for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { float diff = smem_o[h] - mean; var += diff * diff; } float inv_std = rsqrtf(var / H + 1e-5f); for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { smem_o[h] = (smem_o[h] - mean) * inv_std * norm_out_w[h] + norm_out_b[h]; } } __syncthreads(); // Output linear: H -> D_pair if (tid < D_pair) { int d = tid; float val = out_b[d]; for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { val += smem_o[h] * out_w[d * H + h]; } smem_out[d] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Gate from original pair[b,i,j,:] const float* pair_ij = pair + (((b * N + i) * N + j) * D_pair); if (tid < D_pair) { int d = tid; float gate = gate_b[d]; for (int h = 0; h < D_pair; h++) { gate += pair_ij[h] * gate_w[d * D_pair + h]; } gate = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gate)); out_pair[(((b * N + i) * N + j) * D_pair) + d] = pair_ij[d] + gate * smem_out[d]; } } ``` Wait, TriMul issues: 1. The LayerNorm on `o`: I'm using only H threads (32), but H=32 so that's one warp. Other threads idle. Fine. 2. The norm should compute mean and var across all H elements. With 32 threads, warp 0 can do this. But I have `if (tid < H)` and then each thread reads all elements. This is redundant but okay for H=32. Actually only tid=0 should compute mean and var, then broadcast? Or all threads can compute the same thing and write to smem_o. But with `if (tid < H)`, threads 0..31 all enter. They all compute the same thing and write to smem_o. That's fine. __syncthreads() is needed after because other threads might read smem_o later. 3. The output linear uses `if (tid < D_pair)`. D_pair=64. If blockDim.x=128, then 64 threads enter. Fine. But wait, smem_out is shared among all threads. threads 0..63 write to smem_out. Then __syncthreads(). Then gate compute reads smem_out and pair_ij and writes to out_pair. This is fine. 4. However, Pair update: should be `out_pair[b,i,j,:] = pair[b,i,j,:] + gate * smem_out` In the code: `out_pair[...] = pair_ij[d] + gate * smem_out[d]` But `out_pair` points to the original `pair` tensor since we're updating in-place. Wait, the PyTorch code does: `pair_repr = pair_repr + self.tri_mul(pair_repr)`. So we can write back to the same pair tensor (in-place addition). But note: pair_ij is the ORIGINAL pair value (before trimul). In my code, `pair_ij` reads from `pair` at the same address we plan to write `out_pair`. If we read from `pair` and write to `out_pair` (which is the same pointer), and since all threads in the grid access disjoint (b,i,j) positions, there's no race condition. `pair_ij[d]` is read before `out_pair[...]` is written. Let me now carefully review my kernels. **MSA Attention Kernel Details:** 1. Shared memory: `extern __shared__ float smem[];` - smem_msa: N * D elements - smem_attn: N * D elements - smem_k: num_warps * N * head_dim - smem_v: num_warps * N * head_dim Size: (2 * N * D + 2 * num_warps * N * head_dim) * sizeof(float) 2. Block: 128 threads = 4 warps. Grid: (B, S) 3. Loading msa: all threads load from `msa_ptr`. `msa_ptr` points to `msa + ((b * S + s) * N) * D`. Thread `tid` loads elements `tid, tid+128, tid+256, ...` up to `N*D`. Thread 0 loads elements 0, 128, 256, ... These are contiguous in memory: element 0 is at offset 0, element 128 is at offset 128*4 = 512 bytes. Within a warp, threads load adjacent elements in each iteration? Iter 0: thread 0 loads offset 0, thread 1 loads offset 1, ..., thread 31 loads offset 31. Coalesced. Iter 1: thread 0 loads offset 128, thread 1 loads offset 129, etc. Coalesced. Good. 4. LayerNorm: `if (tid < 32) { int n = tid; ... }` Thread n computes mean, var of row n. `__syncthreads()` after. 5. Q,K,V computation: `int h = warp_id;` with warp_id = tid / 32. `int n = lane;` with lane = tid % 32. For each head h (0..3): Thread n computes q_vec[head_dim], k_vec stored in smem_k, v_vec stored in smem_v. The dot products: ```cuda for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) { float s = 0.0f; const float* w = qw + (h * head_dim + dh) * D; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { s += smem_msa[n * D + d] * w[d]; } q_vec[dh] = s; } ``` Q: stored in registers as q_vec[16]. Good. K: written to `smem_k[warp_id * N * head_dim + n * head_dim + dh]`. V: written to `smem_v[warp_id * N * head_dim + n * head_dim + dh]`. Then `__syncwarp()`. 6. Scores: ```cuda for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) { dot += q_vec[dh] * smem_k[warp_id * N * head_dim + m * head_dim + dh]; } scores[m] = dot * scale; } ``` Each thread n computes row n of the attention matrix. 7. Softmax: ```cuda float max_score = scores[0]; for (int m = 1; m < N; m++) max_score = fmaxf(max_score, scores[m]); float exp_sum = 0.0f; for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { scores[m] = expf(scores[m] - max_score); exp_sum += scores[m]; } float inv_es = 1.0f / exp_sum; for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) scores[m] *= inv_es; ``` Standard softmax. No numerical issue since we subtract max. 8. Attn @ V: ```cuda for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) o_vec[dh] = 0.0f; for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) { float s = scores[m]; for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) { o_vec[dh] += s * smem_v[warp_id * N * head_dim + m * head_dim + dh]; } } ``` 9. Write to smem_attn: ```cuda for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) { smem_attn[n * D + h * head_dim + dh] = o_vec[dh]; } ``` Wait, `smem_attn` is shared among all warps. All 4 warps write to it. Warp 0 writes columns 0..15 for all rows. Warp 1 writes columns 16..31 for all rows. ... Each warp writes 32 rows * 16 cols = 512 elements. No bank conflicts if columns of different heads map to different banks. Actually, each thread writes `smem_attn[n * D + h * head_dim + dh]`. Thread n in warp h accesses offset `n*D + h*head_dim + dh`. For warp 0 (h=0): offsets `n*64 + 0..15`. For warp 1 (h=1): offsets `n*64 + 16..31`. These are all different addresses. No conflicts. `__syncthreads()` after all warps write. 10. Output linear + residual: ```cuda for (int i = tid; i < N * D; i += blockDim.x) { int n = i / D; int d_out = i % D; float s = ob[d_out]; const float* w = ow + d_out * D; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { s += smem_attn[n * D + d] * w[d]; } out_msa[...] = msa_ptr[i] + s; } ``` Wait: `ow + d_out * D` is row d_out of ow. That's `ow[d_out, :]`. Dot product with `smem_attn[n, :]`. This computes `y = x @ W^T + b` where x is row n of attn output. Correct. One potential issue: `msa_ptr[i]` reads the original msa. `i = n*D + d_out`. In the first load loop, threads loaded in stride pattern. `msa_ptr[i]` for i from tid to N*D stepping by blockDim.x: these reads might not be perfectly coalesced, but with small data and L1, fine. Actually, thread 0 reads i=0, 128, 256... Thread 1 reads i=1, 129, 257... These are scattered. But with 8KB total and L1, it doesn't matter much. Another thing: in the MSA attention output linear, I'm using `ow + d_out * D`. But the weight shape is (D, D). `ow[d_out, :]` means `ow + d_out * D`. Since D=msa_dim=64, this is correct. Let me assemble the code. Actually, I need to also handle the case where the input tensors might not be contiguous. I'll add `.contiguous()` in forward() to be safe. This has a small cost but ensures correctness. Wait, there's a potential issue with in-place operations in OPM and TriMul. OPM does `pair += opm_result`. TriMul does `pair += trimul_result`. If I launch OPM kernel first (which reads and writes pair), then TriMul kernel (which reads from pair including the OPM update, and writes back), this is correct. But for OPM kernel, I read `pair_ptr[d_out]` and add to it. This is atomic-free because each block handles a unique (b,i,j). For TriMul kernel, I read `pair_ij` (original value before trimul) and compute `new = pair_ij + gate * out`. Then write back. This is also race-free because each block handles unique (b,i,j). But note: TriMul reads `pair_i_base` and `pair_j_base` which are other positions in the same pair tensor. These are not modified by TriMul blocks (since each block only writes its own (i,j)). So no race. Similarly, OPM reads msa which is not modified during OPM. All good. Let me write the complete code now. I'll use triple quotes and be very careful. assistantI'll start by writing the complete fused CUDA kernel implementation. The key insight is that PyTorch's reference implementation launches ~20 separate kernels for this small model, and most of the time is kernel-launch overhead. By fusing each major stage into a single CUDA kernel, we can dramatically reduce latency. Let me write the code and compile it:

When using `load_inline`, you typically need to include forward declarations of your CUDA functions in `cpp_sources`. But actually, looking at torch documentation, `load_inline` expects: - `cpp_sources`: C++ source code (contents of main.cpp) - `cuda_sources`: CUDA source code (contents of cuda.cu) - `functions`: list of function names The auto-generated pybind module wraps the `functions`. For this to work, the functions need to be declared in the `.cpp` file scope. So I need to add forward declarations in `cpp_sources`. MSARowSelfAttention(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, msa_dim, num_heads):\n super().__init__()\n self.num_heads = num_heads\n self.head_dim = msa_dim // num_heads\n self.norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim)\n self.q = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)\n self.k = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)\n self.v = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim, bias=False)\n self.out = nn.Linear(msa_dim, msa_dim)\n\n def forward(self, msa):\n B, S, N, D = msa.shape\n h, d = self.num_heads, self.head_dim\n x = self.norm(msa)\n q = self.q(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)\n k = self.k(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)\n v = self.v(x).view(B, S, N, h, d).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4)\n attn = (q @ k.transpose(-1, -2)) / math.sqrt(d)\n attn = F.softmax(attn, dim=-1)\n out = (attn @ v).permute(0, 1, 3, 2, 4).reshape(B, S, N, D)\n return self.out(out)\n\n\nclass OuterProductMeanSmall(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, msa_dim, pair_dim, hidden=32):\n super().__init__()\n self.norm = nn.LayerNorm(msa_dim)\n self.a = nn.Linear(msa_dim, hidden)\n self.b = nn.Linear(msa_dim, hidden)\n self.out = nn.Linear(hidden * hidden, pair_dim)\n\n def forward(self, msa):\n x = self.norm(msa)\n a = self.a(x)\n b = self.b(x)\n outer = torch.einsum(\"bsih,bsjk->bijhk\", a, b)\n S = msa.shape[1]\n outer = outer / max(S, 1)\n B, N1, N2, H1, H2 = outer.shape\n return self.out(outer.reshape(B, N1, N2, H1 * H2))\n\n\nclass TriMulOut(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, pair_dim, hidden):\n super().__init__()\n self.norm = nn.LayerNorm(pair_dim)\n self.pa = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden)\n self.pb = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden)\n self.ga = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden)\n self.gb = nn.Linear(pair_dim, hidden)\n self.norm_out = nn.LayerNorm(hidden)\n self.out = nn.Linear(hidden, pair_dim)\n self.gate_out = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim)\n\n def forward(self, pair):\n x = self.norm(pair)\n a = self.pa(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.ga(x))\n b = self.pb(x) * torch.sigmoid(self.gb(x))\n o = torch.einsum(\"bikh,bjkh->bijh\", a, b)\n o = self.out(self.norm_out(o))\n return torch.sigmoid(self.gate_out(pair)) * o\n\n\ncpp_declarations = r'''\ntorch::Tensor msa_attn_cuda(\n torch::Tensor Kernel 1: Fused MSA Row Self-Attention\n// ---------------------------------------------------------------------------\n__global__ void msa_attn_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ msa,\n float* __restrict__ out_msa,\n int B, int S, int N, int D, int num_heads, int head_dim,\n const float* __restrict__ norm_w,\n const float* __restrict__ norm_b,\n const float* __restrict__ qw,\n const float* __restrict__ kw,\n const float* __restrict__ vw,\n const float* __restrict__ ow,\n const float* __restrict__ ob)\n{\n extern __shared__ float smem[];\n float* smem_msa = smem;\n float* smem_attn = smem + N * D;\n float* smem_k_base = smem + 2 * N * D;\n float* smem_v_base = smem + 2 * N * D + (blockDim.x / 32) * N * head_dim;\n\n int b = blockIdx.x;\n int s = blockIdx.y;\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n int warp_id = tid / 32;\n int lane = tid % 32;\n\n const float* msa_ptr = msa + (((b * S + s) * N) * D);\n\n // Load msa into smem_msa\n int total_msa = N * D;\n int msa_per_thread = (total_msa + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x;\n int msa_start = tid * msa_per_thread;\n int msa_end = msa_start + msa_per_thread;\n if (msa_end > total_msa) msa_end = total_msa;\n for (int i = msa_start; i < msa_end; i++) {\n smem_msa[i] = msa_ptr[i];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // LayerNorm per row (32 threads handle N rows)\n if (tid < 32) {\n int n = tid;\n float sum = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) {\n sum += smem_msa[n * D + d];\n }\n float mean = sum / D;\n float var_sum = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) {\n float diff = smem_msa[n * D + d] - mean;\n var_sum += diff * diff;\n }\n float inv_std = rsqrtf(var_sum / D + 1e-5f);\n #pragma unroll\n for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) {\n smem_msa[n * D + d] = (smem_msa[n * D + d] - mean) * inv_std * norm_w[d] + norm_b[d];\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Per-head attention\n int h = warp_id;\n if (h < num_heads) {\n int n = lane;\n float q_vec[16];\n float scores[32];\n float o_vec[16];\n float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim);\n\n float* my_k = smem_k_base + warp_id * N * head_dim;\n float* my_v = smem_v_base + warp_id * N * head_dim;\n\n // Compute Q\n #pragma unroll\n for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) {\n float sum = 0.0f;\n const float* w = qw + (h * head_dim + dh) * D;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) {\n sum += smem_msa[n * D + d] * w[d];\n }\n q_vec[dh] = sum;\n }\n // Compute K\n #pragma unroll\n for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) {\n float sum = 0.0f;\n const float* w = kw + (h * head_dim + dh) * D;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) {\n sum += smem_msa[n * D + d] * w[d];\n }\n my_k[n * head_dim + dh] = sum;\n }\n // Compute V\n #pragma unroll\n for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) {\n float sum = 0.0f;\n const float* w = vw + (h * head_dim + dh) * D;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) {\n sum += smem_msa[n * D + d] * w[d];\n }\n my_v[n * head_dim + dh] = sum;\n }\n __syncwarp();\n\n // Q @ K^T\n #pragma unroll\n for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) {\n float dot = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) {\n dot += q_vec[dh] * my_k[m * head_dim + dh];\n }\n scores[m] = dot * scale;\n }\n\n // Softmax\n float max_score = scores[0];\n #pragma unroll\n for (int m = 1; m < N; m++) {\n max_score = fmaxf(max_score, scores[m]);\n }\n float exp_sum = 0.0f;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) {\n scores[m] = expf(scores[m] - max_score);\n exp_sum += scores[m];\n }\n float inv_es = 1.0f / exp_sum;\n #pragma unroll\n for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) {\n scores[m] *= inv_es;\n }\n\n // Attn @ V\n #pragma unroll\n for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) {\n o_vec[dh] = 0.0f;\n }\n #pragma unroll\n for (int m = 0; m < N; m++) {\n float s_val = scores[m];\n #pragma unroll\n for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) {\n o_vec[dh] += s_val * my_v[m * head_dim + dh];\n }\n }\n\n // Write to smem_attn\n #pragma unroll\n for (int dh = 0; dh < head_dim; dh++) {\n smem_attn[n * D + h * head_dim + dh] = o_vec[dh];\n }\n }\n __syncthreads();\n\n // Output linear + residual\n int out_per_thread = (total_msa + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x;\n int out_start = tid * out_per_thread;\n int out_end = out_start + out_per_thread;\n if (out_end > total_msa) out_end = total_msa;\n for (int i = out_start; i < out_end; i++) {\n int n = i / D;\n int d_out = i % D;\n float acc = ob[d_out];\n const float* float* __restrict__ bb,\n const float* __restrict__ ow,\n const float* __restrict__ ob)\n{\n extern __shared__ float smem[];\n float* smem_i = smem;\n float* smem_j = smem + S * D_msa;\n float* smem_a 2 * S;\n int ln_per_thread = (ln_rows + blockDim.x - 1) / blockDim.x;\n int ln_start = tid * ln_per_thread;\n int ln_end = ln_start + ln_per_thread;\n if (ln_end > ln_rows) ln_end = ln_rows;\n for
